5 min
Prophet Abraham's Argument Against Star-Worship
Sanusi explains Abraham's dialogue as a proof of contingency and asserts prophetic infallibility.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that Prophet Abraham's statement 'This is my Lord' was a rhetorical argument to demonstrate the contingency of celestial bodies, and that prophets are infallible from disbelief and sin.

Core Idea
Abraham's 'This is my Lord' was a debate tactic, not a belief.

This lesson explores Sanusi's explanation of the Quranic account of Prophet Abraham's debate with his star-worshipping people. Sanusi emphasizes that Abraham's words were a proof of contingency, not a confession of belief, and he defends prophetic infallibility against common misunderstandings. Through careful analysis, we see how the choice of setting over rising as evidence reveals the subtlety of prophetic teaching.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation once to get the flow of Sanusi's argument. Notice the Quranic verses he cites and how he structures his explanation.
The passage is dense; reading twice may help.
2
Identify the two interpretations
Sanusi gives two possible meanings for Abraham's statement: either it is a rhetorical question with the interrogative omitted, or it is a quotation of the opponent's claim to refute it. Both preserve prophetic infallibility.
3
Understand the role of setting vs. rising
Sanusi explains why Abraham waited for the star to set before using it as evidence. The setting involves change and diminution, making the argument clearer, while the rising might dazzle a foolish person.
Notice the Arabic terms: أفول (setting) and طلوع (rising).
4
Reflect on the misconception
Consider the common misunderstanding that Abraham doubted or actually believed in the star's lordship. How does Sanusi refute it using prophetic infallibility?
This connects to the broader doctrine of 'ismah.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما قوله جل وعلا في حقٌّ إبراهيم عليه الصلاةُ والسلام : ﴿فَلَمَّا جَنَّ عَلَيْهِ الَّيْلُ رَمَا كَوْكَبَا قَالَ هَذَا رَبِّي ... ﴾ [الأنعام : ٧٦] إلى آخره : فهو إقامة منه عليه الصلاة والسلامُ الدلالة لقومه على حدوث هذهِ العلويَّاتِ التي عبدَها قومُهُ وادَّعَوا لها الألوهيَّةَ ، ولذلك قال جلَّ مِنْ قائل : ﴿ وَتِلْكَ حُجَّتُنَا وَآتَيْنَهَا إِبْرَاهِيمَ عَلَى قَوْمِهِ ... [الأنعام : ٨٣] الآية ، لا أَنَّهُ عليهِ الصلاة والسلام كان يعتقد ربوبيتها أو يشكُ فيها ، وعند إقامة هذا الدليل زالَ عنه ذلك الاعتقاد أوِ الشكُ ! كما توهَّمَهُ كثيرٌ ممَّنْ لا خلاقَ لهُ ممَّنْ يدَّعي التصوف وغيرَه ؛ لأنَّ الأنبياء عليهم السلامُ معصومونَ مِنَ الكفر قبل النبوة وبعـدهـا ، في صغرهم وكبرهم ، بل هم معصومونَ مِنْ جميع المعاصي صغيرها وكبيرها عموماً على ما سبق تحقيقه (١) فمعنى قوله عليه الصلاة والسلام : ﴿هَذَا رَبِّي﴾ [الأنعام : ٧٦] : أهذا ربي ؟! على ما تزعمون ، بحذفِ حرفِ الاستفهام ، أو مِنْ بابِ ذكرِ دعوى الخصم لإقامة البرهان على إبطالها وطلوع هذه الكواكب بعد أن لم تكن هو في الاستدلال به على حدوثها كالأفول ، إلا أنَّهُ عليهِ الصلاة والسلامُ إِنَّما أَخَّرَ الاستدلال على حدوثها إلى رؤيةِ أُفولِها لِمَا في الأفولِ مِنَ التغيرِ بالنقصانِ ، فدلالته على حدوثِ تلك الكواكب وعدم صلاحيتها للربوبية واضح للذكي والغبي ، أمَّا طلوعها - وإنْ كانَ دليلاً على حدوثها مِنْ ناحية تجدُّدِهِ بعدَ أنْ لم يكن - فلأنَّهُ لمَّا كانَ فيهِ كمال لها لِمَا صاحبَهُ مِنْ تلكَ الأنوار التي تُوجَدُ لذاتِ النفس والامتدادِ إليها بالأبصار .. قد تُسكِرُ عقل الغبي الشهواني المقلَّدِ أوِ المعاندِ (٢) ، فلا يَتأَمَّلُ في وجهِ دلالتِهِ على الحدوثِ ، ولا يصغي لسماعها
As for His saying, Glorious and Exalted, regarding Abraham, peace and blessings be upon him: {When the night covered him, he saw a star and said: 'This is my Lord'...} [Al-An'am 6:76] to its end: it is an establishment by him, peace and blessings be upon him, of proof to his people for the contingency of these celestial bodies which his people worshipped and claimed divinity for them. Therefore, He said, Glorious is He, {And that was Our proof which We gave Abraham against his people} [Al-An'am 6:83] the verse. Not that he, peace and blessings be upon him, believed in their lordship or doubted it, and that at the establishment of this proof that belief or doubt left him, as many, with no good share, who claim Sufism and others, have imagined. Because the prophets, peace be upon them, are infallible from disbelief before prophethood and after it, in their youth and old age. Rather, they are infallible from all sins, small and large, generally, as previously verified (1). So the meaning of his statement, peace and blessings be upon him: {This is my Lord} [Al-An'am 6:76] is: 'Is this my Lord?!' according to your claim, by omission of the interrogative particle, or it is from the type of mentioning the opponent's claim to establish the proof of its invalidation. And the rising of these stars after they were not is, in evidence for their contingency, like the setting, except that he, peace and blessings be upon him, delayed the evidence for their contingency until seeing their setting because of the change by diminution in the setting. So its evidence for the contingency of those stars and their unsuitability for lordship is clear to the intelligent and the stupid. As for their rising—although it is evidence for their contingency from the aspect of its renewal after it was not—because it contains perfection for them due to what accompanies it of those lights which are found for the soul's pleasure and the eyes' extension towards them, it may intoxicate the mind of the base, lustful, imitative or obstinate person (2), so he does not contemplate the aspect of its evidence for contingency, nor does he listen to it.
Source pages p. 228

Commentary
This passage is an explanation of the Quranic account of Prophet Abraham's debate with his people. Sanusi begins by stating that Abraham's statement 'This is my Lord' regarding the star was not a declaration of belief but an argument (إقامة دلالة) to prove the contingency (حدوث) of celestial bodies that his people worshipped. Sanusi cites the verse where Allah confirms that this was a proof given to Abraham (الأنعام 83). He then refutes those who claim that Abraham actually believed in or doubted the lordship of the stars, emphasizing that prophets are infallible (معصومون) from disbelief and all sins before and after prophethood, as previously established (referencing page 190). He explains that the meaning of Abraham's words is either a rhetorical question ('Is this my Lord?') with the interrogative omitted, or a quotation of the opponent's claim to refute it. Sanusi then compares the rising (طلوع) and setting (أفول) of stars as evidence for their contingency. While both indicate contingency, Abraham delayed the proof to the moment of setting because setting involves change by diminution, making the argument more accessible to all, whereas rising is accompanied by beauty and light that may intoxicate the mind of a foolish or obstinate person, preventing them from understanding the evidence.

Key Terms
استدلال (argument/proof)
حدوث (contingency/occurrence)
أفول (setting)
طلوع (rising)
عصمة (infallibility)
ربوبية (lordship)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Prophet Abraham actually believed in or doubted the lordship of the star before receiving proof.
Correction: Abraham was infallible from disbelief and his statement was a rhetorical tool, not a confession of belief or doubt.

Checkpoint
Why did Prophet Abraham say 'This is my Lord' when seeing a star?
A) He truly believed the star was his lord.
B) He was testing his own faith.
C) He was presenting his people's claim to refute it.
D) He was confused at that time.

Key Takeaway
Prophet Abraham's words were a rhetorical argument to prove the contingency of the stars, and prophets are infallible from disbelief and sin.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you see something beautiful in creation—a sunset, a talent, a possession—do you ever feel a pull to attach your heart to it as if it were ultimate? How does Abraham's example of looking past the star's beauty to its impermanence challenge you to reorient your attachments today? What one attachment will you consciously place below Allah before you sleep tonight?
?
The text says that the rising star 'may intoxicate the mind of the base, lustful, imitative or obstinate person.' Which of these four describes a tendency in your own heart when you encounter evidence of Allah's power? What specific change will you make this week to prevent that tendency from blocking your recognition of truth?
?
Prophets never doubted, but you and I do. When a doubt about Allah's existence or His plan crosses your mind, do you feel the same urgency to 'delay' the evidence until it becomes crystal clear, as Abraham waited for the setting? Or do you settle for a superficial faith that is easily shaken? What concrete step will you take to strengthen your certainty so that it endures even when trials arise?

Musa's Fear: For Allah's Sake, Not Personal Harm
Sanusi explains that Musa's fear was for Allah's sake and zeal for Tawhid, not fear of harm.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that Prophet Musa's fear when facing the magicians was for the sake of Allah and out of zeal for Tawhid, not due to personal fear of harm.

Core Idea
Musa's fear was for Allah's cause, not for himself.

This lesson examines a verse from Surah Taha where Prophet Musa experiences fear before the magicians. Sanusi clarifies that this fear was not from cowardice but from concern for the clear demonstration of Allah's signs and the preservation of true guidance. The passage also relates an incident involving Gabriel's address to the magicians, which explains the source of Musa's concern.

Guided Path
1
Read the source passage
Read the Arabic passage and its literal translation. Notice the verse quoted and the explanation that follows.
2
Understand the primary explanation
Sanusi states that Musa's fear was for Allah and out of zeal for Tawhid. He feared his miracle might be obscured by the magicians' miracle.
3
Examine the reported reason
The passage includes an additional explanation: Musa heard Gabriel address the magicians as 'friends of Allah', causing fear that this might be misinterpreted.
This shows the depth of Musa's concern for guidance.
4
Reflect on the lesson's significance
Consider that prophets' fears are always for divine matters, not personal safety. This corrects a common misunderstanding.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما قوله تعالى في حق موسى عليه الصلاة والسلام معَ السَّحَرَةِ : فَأَوْجَسَ فِي نَفْسِهِ خِيفَةً مُوسَى ﴾ [طه : ٦٧] : فخوفُهُ عليهِ السلامُ إِنَّمَا كَانَ لأجل الله ، وغيرةً على توحيدِهِ ، خافَ ألا تتضح للحاضرين دلالة معجزتِهِ معَ خارقهم ، وقد قيل : إن سبب خوفِهِ عليهِ السلامُ أَنَّهُ سمعَ جبريل عليه الصلاةُ والسلامُ يقولُ للسَّحَرَةِ عند إلقائهم حبالهم وعصيَّهم : القُوا يا أولياءَ اللهِ ، فخافَ مِنْ قولِهِ لهم : ( يا أولياء الله ) أن يكون ذلكَ علامةً لظهورِ خارقِهم للحاضرين ، فيتمادوا على الضلالة
And as for His statement, exalted be He, regarding Moses, peace be upon him, with the magicians: 'Then Moses conceived in himself a fear' [Taha: 67]: His fear, peace be upon him, was only for the sake of Allah and out of zeal for His tawhid. He feared that the evidence of his miracle might not become clear to those present alongside their miracle. And it was said: The reason for his fear, peace be upon him, is that he heard Gabriel, peace be upon him, saying to the magicians when they threw their ropes and staffs: 'Throw, O friends of Allah,' so he feared from his saying 'O friends of Allah' that this would become a sign of the manifestation of their miracle to those present, so they would persist in misguidance.
Source pages p. 229

Commentary
This passage explains the verse describing Moses' fear when facing the magicians. The author clarifies that Moses' fear was not for his personal safety or due to cowardice, but rather it was 'for the sake of Allah' and out of zealous protectiveness for tawhid (monotheism). Specifically, he feared that the clear evidence of his own miracle might be obscured for the onlookers by the apparent miracle of the magicians. The author then reports an additional explanation: that Moses heard Gabriel addressing the magicians as 'O friends of Allah' when they cast their ropes and staffs. This address could be misinterpreted as a sign of divine favor towards the magicians, leading the people to continue in their disbelief. Thus, Moses' fear was purely a concern for the correct understanding of divine signs and the guidance of the people.

Key Terms
الخيفة
الغيرة
التوحيد
المعجزة
الخارق
الضلالة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think Moses feared physical harm or defeat.
Correction: The author emphasizes his fear was for Allah's sake and the preservation of true guidance.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what was the primary reason for Prophet Musa's fear when facing the magicians?
A) He feared that the magicians would harm him physically.
B) He feared that the people would not recognize his miracle due to the magicians' miracle.
C) He feared that Allah was displeased with him.
D) He feared that his brother Harun would be harmed.

Key Takeaway
Prophet Musa's fear before the magicians was motivated by his zeal for Tawhid and concern for the guidance of the people, not by personal fear of harm.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you see others succeeding in ways that appear to contradict Islamic values, do you feel a protective zeal for tawhid, or are you secretly envious? What does Musa's example teach you about the purity of your concern?
?
Are you more afraid of personal embarrassment when your faith is challenged, or of the possibility that others might be led astray? How can you shift your fear to be for Allah's sake alone?
?
If you heard someone being praised in a way that could misrepresent Islam, would you speak up or stay silent? What is the cost of remaining silent for the guidance of those around you?

Prophethood

Proving the Prophethood of Muhammad (ṣ)
Sanusi presents a concise proof of Muhammad's prophethood through claim and miracle.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the two-part proof for Muhammad's prophethood based on his claim and his miracle.

Core Idea
A true prophet is known by his claim and his miracle.

Sanusi opens with a firm declaration that Muhammad's prophethood is certain for those guided by God. He then sets up the proof: anyone who claims prophethood and performs a miracle is a true prophet. This lesson unpacks that syllogism and the evidence for each part.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the translation. Notice the emphatic opening and the structure.
Pay attention to the footnote at the end.
2
Identify the two components of the proof
The proof has two parts: Muhammad's claim to prophethood and his manifestation of a miracle. The author states that whoever has both is a true prophet.
This is a syllogism with two premises.
3
Examine the evidence for each part
For the claim, the author says it is known by necessity (bi-ḍarūrah) from history. For the miracle, he cites the Quran and prophecies about hidden things.
These are the specific evidences from the passage.
4
Reflect on the refutation of objectors
The author mentions that only some Jews and Christians reject this proof. Ask yourself: does the syllogism adequately refute them?
The proof is meant to be conclusive.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
لاريب ولاخفاء لكلِّ مُوفّق : أن سيدنا ومولانا محمداً صلى الله عليه وسلَّمَ رسولُ اللهِ تعالى ، أرسله جل وعلا بالهدى ودين الحق لكافة الإنس والجن ، وجعل سبحانه شريعته السمحة ناسخة لجميع الشرائع باقية إلى أن تقوم الساعةُ ، ولم يخالف في ثبوتِ رسالتِهِ عليه الصلاة والسلامُ مِنْ أهل الملل والأديان إلا البعضُ مِنَ اليهود والنصارى . والحُجَّةُ عليهم : أَنَّهُ عليهِ الصلاةُ والسلامُ ادَّعى النبوَّةَ والرسالة ، وأظهر المعجزة ، وكلُّ مَنْ كانَ كذلك فهو نبي رسول أما دعواه عليه الصلاة والسلام الرسالة إلى الخلق : فأمر معلوم بالضرورة . وأمَّا إظهاره للمعجزة : فلأنَّهُ أتى بالقرآن ، وأخبر بالمغيَّباتِ ، (۱) في هامش ( د ) : ( الضمير في ( أفضلهم » يعود على الملائكة والأنبياء )
There is no doubt nor concealment for every one granted success: that our master and liege Muhammad, may God bless him and grant him peace, is the Messenger of God Almighty. He, glorified and exalted, sent him with guidance and the religion of truth to all of mankind and jinn, and made his tolerant law abrogating all previous laws, remaining until the Hour is established. And none among the people of religions and faiths opposed the establishment of his message, peace be upon him, except some of the Jews and Christians. The proof against them is that he, peace be upon him, claimed prophethood and messengership, and manifested the miracle; and everyone who is like that is a prophet and messenger. As for his claim, peace be upon him, of messengership to creation: it is a matter known by necessity. As for his manifestation of the miracle: because he brought the Quran and informed of hidden things. (1) In the margin of (d): 'The pronoun in 'afḍaluhum' refers to the angels and the prophets.'
Source pages p. 233

Commentary
This passage is a concise proof of the prophethood of Muhammad. The author begins with an emphatic assertion that it is beyond doubt for the divinely guided person. He then explains the universality of Muhammad's mission: sent to all humans and jinn, his law abrogates all previous laws and remains until Judgment Day. He notes that only some Jews and Christians reject his prophethood. The proof against them is a two-part syllogism: (1) Muhammad claimed prophethood and performed miracles; (2) whoever claims prophethood and performs a miracle is a true prophet. The evidence for his claim is that it is known necessarily (bi-ḍarūrah) from his biography and the historical record. The evidence for his miracle is the Quran itself and his prophecies of future events. The footnote clarifies that the word 'afḍaluhum' (best of them) in the title refers to the angels and prophets, meaning Muhammad is the best of all creation.

Key Terms
الرسالة
النبوّة
المعجزة
الضرورة
القرآن
المغيَّبات

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Miracles alone are not sufficient proof for prophethood.
Correction: Sanusi argues that a claim plus a miracle together constitute the standard criterion for identifying a true prophet.

Checkpoint
According to this passage, what two things constitute the proof of Muhammad's prophethood?
A) His honesty and his knowledge
B) His claim and his miracle
C) His lineage and his wealth
D) His conquests and his followers

Key Takeaway
Muhammad's prophethood is proven by his claim and his manifest miracle, namely the Quran and his prophecies.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The lesson begins by saying there is no doubt for 'every one granted success' (muwaffaq). Are you truly among that group, or do you harbor hidden doubts about Muhammad's prophethood? What would it take for you to become one of the muwaffaqūn today?
?
You just read that the Quran is a miracle from God. When you recite or listen to the Quran, do you sense that it is a direct communication from the Creator, or is it just another text? What one change can you make in how you approach the Quran this week so that it becomes a living proof for you?
?
The author mentions that only some Jews and Christians rejected the Prophet. But what about you? Does your belief in Muhammad (ṣ) affect how you treat others, how you spend your time, and what you prioritize? If not, what is missing in your conviction, and what will you do to make it real?

Prophethood

Proofs of Muhammad's Prophethood
Author proves Muhammad's prophethood through miracles and scriptural testimony.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Sanusi proves the prophethood of Muhammad (peace be upon him) through a combination of his claim, his miracles (especially the Qur'an), and scriptural prophecies.

Core Idea
Prophetic proof combines claim, miracle, and scripture — each reinforcing the other.

Sanusi begins by establishing the certainty of Muhammad's prophethood, acknowledging that only a few reject it. He then presents a concise twofold proof: the prophet's own claim (known necessarily) and his manifestation of miracles, which he supports with examples and scriptural testimony. This passage weaves together rational evidence and prior revelation, showing the comprehensive nature of Islamic prophetic proof.

Guided Path
1
Identify the twofold proof
Read the passage and find where Sanusi states the two elements that constitute the proof: the claim of prophethood and the manifestation of miracles.
The author explicitly says 'the proof against them is that he claimed... and he manifested the miracle.'
2
Understand the role of tawātur
Notice that the miracles are said to reach the level of tawātur (mass transmission), which makes them certain and undeniable.
The author mentions that the miracles are beyond enumeration and known through mass transmission.
3
Examine the scriptural testimony
See how Sanusi uses previous scriptures (Torah) as additional evidence, giving one example from the fifth book.
The passage cites a verse from the Torah interpreted as referring to the giving of Torah, Gospel, and Qur'an.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
لا ريب ولا خفاء لكل موفق: أن سيدنا ومولانا محمداً صلى الله عليه وسلم رسول الله تعالى، أرسله جل وعلا بالهدى ودين الحق لكافة الإنس والجن، وجعل سبحانه شريعته السمحة ناسخة لجميع الشرائع باقية إلى أن تقوم الساعة، ولم يخالف في ثبوت رسالته عليه الصلاة والسلام من أهل الملل والأديان إلا البعض من اليهود والنصارى. والحجة عليهم: أنه عليه الصلاة والسلام ادعى النبوة والرسالة، وأظهر المعجزة، وكل من كان كذلك فهو نبي رسول. أما دعواه عليه الصلاة والسلام الرسالة إلى الخلق: فأمر معلوم بالضرورة. وأما إظهاره للمعجزة: فلأنه أتى بالقرآن، وأخبر بالمغيبات، وأظهر أفعالاً كثيرة تخرج عن الحصر على خلاف المعتاد، بلغت جملتها حد التواتر. واستيفاء ذلك مما لا تفي به الأسفار الكبيرة، ولا التصانيف الطويلة، وكل ذلك زيادة على النصوص الدالة على نبوته وعظيم شرفه الوارد في كتب الأنبياء المتقدمين عليهم الصلاة والسلام، المنقولة إلى القرى المشهورة فيما بين أممهم، وهي نصوص كثيرة جداً، كافية في معرفة نبوته عليه الصلاة والسلام. بعض ما جاء من الأخبار عنه عليه الصلاة والسلام في الكتب المقدسة: منها: ما جاء في السفر الخامس من التوراة: (جاء الله من طور سيناء، وأشرق من ساغين، واستعلن من جبال فاران؛ وذلك كناية عن إنزال الله تعالى التوراة على موسى عليه السلام بطور سيناء والإنجيل على عيسى عليه الصلاة والسلام بساغين، وهو من جبال...
There is no doubt nor concealment for every rightly guided person: that our master Muhammad صلى الله عليه وسلم is the Messenger of Allah Almighty, He sent him with guidance and the religion of truth to all mankind and jinn, and He made his tolerant law abrogating all laws, remaining until the Hour is established. None opposed the establishment of his message from the people of religions except some of the Jews and Christians. The proof against them is that he claimed prophethood and messengership, and he manifested the miracle, and everyone who is like that is a prophet and messenger. As for his claim of messengership to creation: it is a matter known necessarily. And as for his manifestation of the miracle: because he brought the Qur'an, informed of unseen things, and manifested many actions beyond enumeration, contrary to custom, reaching the level of tawātur (mass transmission). Exhausting that is not achieved by large books nor lengthy compositions. All that is in addition to the texts indicating his prophethood and great honor found in the books of the previous prophets, transmitted to the well-known towns among their nations, and they are very numerous texts, sufficient for knowing his prophethood. Some of the reports about him in the sacred books: among them: what came in the fifth book of the Torah: 'Allah came from Mount Sinai, and shone from Sāghīn, and appeared from the mountains of Fārān'; that is a metaphor for Allah sending down the Torah to Moses on Mount Sinai, and the Gospel to Jesus on Sāghīn, and it is from the mountains...
Source pages p. 233

Commentary
This passage is a sustained argument proving the prophethood of Muhammad صلى الله عليه وسلم. It opens by declaring the certainty of his mission for all who are rightly guided, and notes that only some Jews and Christians reject it. The core proof is presented as a two-fold argument: first, Muhammad’s own claim to prophethood is known necessarily (by the fact that he delivered the message and it is self-evident); second, he manifested miracles—specifically the Qur’an, reports of the unseen, and countless supernormal acts that collectively reach the level of tawātur (massively transmitted, leaving no doubt). The author then asserts that the proof from miracles is so extensive that no large book could cover it all, and that these are additional to the prophecies about him in earlier scriptures. He gives one example from the fifth book of the Torah, interpreting the passage as referring to the revelation of the Torah (Mount Sinai), the Gospel (Sāghīn), and the Qur’an or Muhammad’s advent (Mount Paran/Fārān). The argument shows that the author uses both rational (miracle-based) and scriptural proofs.

الرسالة
المعجزة
التواتر
التوراة
الإنجيل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think the proof relies solely on the claim of prophethood, without requiring miracles.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what two elements constitute the proof against those who deny Muhammad's prophethood?
A) His claim alone, and the agreement of all scholars.
B) His claim and his manifestation of miracles.
C) His personal character and the political success of Islam.
D) The beauty of his language and the expansion of his community.

Key Takeaway
The prophethood of Muhammad is proven by his own claim, the manifest miracles that reached tawātur, and the testimonies in previous scriptures.

?

The Prophecy of Paran: A Scriptural Proof of Muhammad's Prophethood
Sanusi presents a Torah prophecy and interprets it as foretelling the revelations to Moses, Jesus, and Muhammad.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Sanusi interprets the Torah verse about God appearing from Paran as a clear prophecy of Prophet Muhammad's mission and the revelation of the Quran.

Core Idea
The Torah itself points to Mecca as the source of final revelation.

Sanusi begins by stating that the Prophet's miracles are numerous and mutawatir, but he then adds that there are explicit scriptural prophecies as further evidence. The first prophecy he presents is from the fifth book of the Torah, which describes God coming from Sinai, shining from Seir, and appearing from Paran. Sanusi explains this as the successive revelations: Torah at Sinai, Gospel at Seir, and Quran at Paran—identified as Mecca. This shows that the Torah foretold Muhammad's prophethood.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic and translation, noting how Sanusi introduces the scriptural proofs.
2
Identify the three locations
Notice the progression: Sinai (Moses), Seir (Jesus), Paran (Muhammad).
3
Understand the metaphor
'God came' means His law came; 'appeared' indicates clarity and miracles.
4
Appreciate the consensus
Sanusi states Paran is Mecca by consensus, strengthening the argument.
This consensus is key to the proof.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأظهر أفعالاً كثيرةً تخرجُ عن الحصرِ على خلاف المعتادِ ، بلغَتْ جملتها حد التواتر . واستيفاء ذلك ممَّا لا تفي بهِ الأسفار الكبيرة ، ولا التصانيف الطويلة ، وكلُّ ذلك زيادة على النصوص الدالة على نبوته وعظيم شرفه الوارد في كتب الأنبياء المتقدمين عليهم الصلاة والسلام ، المنقولة إلى القرى المشهورة فيما بينَ أُممِهم ، وهي نصوص كثيرة جداً ، كافية في معرفة نبوّتِهِ عليه الصلاة والسلام . بعضُ ما جاءَ من الأخبار عنه عليه الصلاة والسلام في الكتب المقدسة : منها : ما جاءَ في السِّفرِ الخامس مِنَ التوراة : ( جَاءَ اللهُ مِنْ طُورِ سيناء ، وأشرقَ مِنْ ساغينَ) ، واستعلنَ مِنْ جبال فاران ((۲) ؛ وذلكَ كناية عن إنزال الله تعالى التوراة على موسى عليه السلام بطُورِ سيناء والإنجيل على عيسى عليه الصلاةُ والسلام بساغينَ ، وهو مِنْ جبالِ الشام ، وأنزل الفرقان على نبينا ومولانا محمد صلى الله عليه وسلَّمَ بجبال فاران . وفاران : مكة بإجماع (۱) ، ومعنى ( جاءَ اللهُ ) : جاءَ شرعُهُ ودينه الحقِّ مِنْ هذه المواضع على أيدي هؤلاء الرُّسُلِ عليهم الصلاة والسلام . وانظر كيف عبر في التوراة عن ظهورِ نبينا ومولانا محمد عليهِ الصلاة والسلام بالاستعلان الذي يقتضي كمال الوضوح والظهورِ ؛ إشارة إلى كثرة معجزاتِ نبيَّنا صلَّى اللهُ عليهِ وسلَّمَ ، وإظهار دينِهِ على جميع الأديان ، وانتشارِهِ وبقائِهِ إلى أن تقوم الساعة . ومنها : ما جاءَ في السِّفرِ الخامس مِنَ التوراةِ : أَنَّه تعالى قالَ لموسى عليه الصلاةُ والسلام : ( إنّي مقيم لبني إسرائيل نبياً مِنْ بني إخوتهم مثلكَ ، وأُجري قولي في فيه ، ويقول لهم ما أمرهم به ، والرجلُ الذي لا يقبلُ قول النبي الذي يتكلم باسمي فأنا أنتقم منه ) ((٢) ، ولا شكّ أنَّ المراد ببني إخوة بني إسرائيل : بنو إسماعيل ؛ لأنهم إخوتهم.
And he manifested many acts beyond enumeration, contrary to the customary, the total of which reached the level of recurrent transmission. And enumerating that is not accomplished by large books nor lengthy writings. And all that is in addition to the texts indicating his prophethood and great honor found in the books of the previous prophets, peace and blessings be upon them, transmitted to the well-known towns among their nations—and they are very many texts, sufficient for knowing his prophethood, peace and blessings be upon him. Some of the reports about him, peace and blessings be upon him, in the sacred books are: among them: what came in the fifth book of the Torah: 'God came from Mount Sinai, and shone forth from Sagine, and appeared from the mountains of Paran'—and that is a metaphor for Allah Almighty sending down the Torah to Moses, peace be upon him, at Mount Sinai, and the Gospel to Jesus, peace and blessings be upon him, at Sagine, which is among the mountains of Syria, and sending down the Criterion to our Prophet and Master Muhammad, may Allah bless him and grant him peace, at the mountains of Paran. And Paran: it is Mecca by consensus [footnote marker], and the meaning of 'God came' is: His law and true religion came from these places at the hands of these messengers, peace and blessings be upon them. And observe how the Torah expressed the appearance of our Prophet and Master Muhammad, peace and blessings be upon him, with 'appearing' which necessitates complete clarity and manifestation—an indication of the abundance of our Prophet's miracles, may Allah bless him and grant him peace, and the manifestation of his religion over all religions, and its spread and its continuance until the Hour is established. And among them: what came in the fifth book of the Torah: that Allah, Exalted be He, said to Moses, peace and blessings be upon him: 'I will raise up for the Children of Israel a prophet from among their brethren like you, and I will put my words in his mouth, and he will say to them what I command them. And the man who does not accept the words of the prophet who speaks in my name, I will take vengeance on him.' And there is no doubt that what is meant by 'the brethren of the Children of Israel' is the Children of Ishmael; because they are their brethren.
Source pages p. 234

Commentary
This passage is a proof of the prophethood of Muhammad (SAW) from previous scriptures. The author begins by summarizing that the Prophet's miracles are numerous and mutawatir (recurrently transmitted), but then states that beyond this, there are explicit scriptural texts in the books of previous prophets that foretell his prophethood. He then gives two examples from the Torah. The first is from Deuteronomy 33:2, which describes God coming from Sinai, shining from Seir (here called Sagine), and appearing from Paran. The author explains this as a metaphor for the revelation of the Torah to Moses at Sinai, the Gospel to Jesus at Seir (in Syria), and the Quran to Muhammad at Paran. He emphasizes that Paran is unanimously identified as Mecca. The phrase 'God came' is interpreted metaphorically as the coming of His law and true religion. The author then notes that the expression 'appeared' for Muhammad indicates clarity and abundance of miracles and the enduring spread of his religion. The second prophecy is from Deuteronomy 18:15-22, where God promises to raise a prophet like Moses from among the brethren of Israel. The author argues that 'brethren' refers to the Ishmaelites (Arabs), since Ishmael is the brother of Isaac, and thus the prophet is Muhammad. This line of argument demonstrates Sanusi's method of using scriptural prophecy as a rational proof for prophethood, relying on authoritative textual interpretation accepted by the community.

Key Terms
التوراة
فاران
الاستعلان

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think these prophecies are vague and open to multiple interpretations.
Correction: Sanusi insists that the identification of Paran as Mecca is by consensus, and the verse explicitly points to the three prophets in order.

Checkpoint

A mountain in Syria

Key Takeaway
The Torah verse about God appearing from Paran is a clear prophecy that the final revelation would come to Prophet Muhammad in Mecca.

?
Sanusi claims this prophecy is known 'by consensus.' Are you content to accept scholarly consensus, or do you need to verify these proofs yourself? What effort will you make to either strengthen your conviction or address your questions?

The Prophet Like Moses: A Torah Promise Fulfilled in Muhammad
Sanusi presents a second Torah prophecy and identifies Muhammad as the promised prophet like Moses.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Sanusi's argument that the prophecy of a prophet like Moses in Deuteronomy 18 refers to Prophet Muhammad, not any other Israelite prophet.

Core Idea
The 'brethren of Israel' are the Ishmaelites, so the promised prophet is Muhammad.

Sanusi presents a second prophecy from the Torah, where God tells Moses He will raise a prophet from among the brethren of the Children of Israel, like Moses. Sanusi identifies these brethren as the Ishmaelites (Arabs), because they are brothers to the Israelites. This leads to the conclusion that the promised prophet is Muhammad, who came from the Ishmaelite lineage and brought a law similar to Moses.

Guided Path
1
Read the prophecy carefully
Note that it is from the fifth book of the Torah, a direct statement from God to Moses.
2
Identify the key phrase
'From among their brethren'—understand who 'their brethren' are.
3
Sanusi's reasoning
The brethren of Bani Israel are Bani Isma'il (Ishmaelites) because they are brothers.
4
Connect to Muhammad
He is from the line of Ishmael, and he is like Moses in bringing a law and facing opposition.
5
Reflect on the simplicity
Sanusi relies on clear lineage and the consensus that the prophecy refers to Muhammad.

Prophecy of Mount Paran: Proof from the Torah
Author explains the Mount Paran prophecy and interprets 'Allah came' metaphorically.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the Torah’s mention of Mount Faran and the phrase 'Allah came' are used as proofs for the prophethood of Muhammad (peace be upon him) and how the term 'isti'lan' points to the clarity and universality of Islam.

Core Idea
The Torah itself foretold Muhammad's coming from Mecca with a manifest religion.

This lesson examines a key proof from the Torah that early Muslim scholars used to demonstrate the truth of Prophet Muhammad’s prophethood. The passage identifies Mount Faran with Mecca, interprets the theophanic language 'Allah came' as the coming of His law, and highlights the significant term 'isti'lan' (manifestation) which conveys the clarity and overwhelming evidence of Muhammad’s mission. Let’s walk through this argument step by step.

Guided Path
1
Identify the proof from Torah
Notice that the passage begins by quoting a Torah verse about 'Allah came from Mount Faran'. Understand that this is being used as a prophecy of Prophet Muhammad.
Mount Faran is identified as Mecca by consensus.
2
Interpret 'Allah came' correctly
The author explains that 'Allah came' does not mean a physical descent, but rather the coming of His law and religion through the messengers.
This refutes anthropomorphism.
3
Analyze the term 'isti'lan'
The passage draws attention to the specific word 'isti'lan' used in the Torah, which implies full clarity and manifestation, linking it to Muhammad's abundant miracles and the worldwide spread of Islam.
4
Understand the broader argument
Reflect on how this prophecy serves as a proof of prophethood by showing that earlier scriptures foretold the coming of Islam.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الشام ، وأنزل الفرقان على نبينا ومولانا محمد صلى الله عليه وسلم بجبال فاران . وفاران : مكة بإجماع (۱) ، ومعنى ( جاء الله ) : جاء شرعه ودينه الحق من هذه المواضع على أيدي هؤلاء الرسل عليهم السلام . وانظر كيف عبر في التوراة عن ظهور نبينا ومولانا محمد عليه الصلاة والسلام بالاستعلان الذي يقتضي كمال الوضوح والظهور ؛ إشارة إلى كثرة معجزات نبينا صلى الله عليه وسلم ، وإظهار دينه على جميع الأديان ، وانتشاره وبقائه إلى أن تقوم الساعة . (۱) قال العلامة ياقوت في معجم البلدان ( ٤ / ٢٢٥ ) : ( فاران : كلمة عبرانية معربة ، وهي من أسماء مكة ، ذكرها في التوراة ، قيل : هو اسم لجبال مكة ) . وقوله : ( هي مكة بإجماع ) يعني : في هذا الموضع ، وإلا فقد ذكر العلامة ياقوت أيضا ( ٢٢٥/٤ ) أنها علم على قرية من نواحي صغد سمرقند ، وكورة من كور مصر القبلية .
Ash-Sham (Syria), and He revealed the Criterion (Al-Furqan) upon our Prophet and our Master Muhammad (peace be upon him) at the mountains of Faran. And Faran is Mecca by consensus. And the meaning of 'Allah came' is: His law and true religion came from these places at the hands of these messengers (peace be upon them). And observe how in the Torah the appearance of our Prophet and our Master Muhammad (peace be upon him) is expressed by the term 'al-isti'lan' (manifestation) which implies complete clarity and manifestation; indicating the abundance of miracles of our Prophet (peace be upon him), and the supremacy of his religion over all religions, and its spread and endurance until the Day of Resurrection. (1) Al-Allamah Yaqut said in Mu'jam al-Buldan (4/225): 'Faran is a Hebrew word, Arabized, and it is one of the names of Mecca, mentioned in the Torah, and it is said: it is the name of the mountains of Mecca.' And his statement: 'it is Mecca by consensus' means in this context; otherwise, al-Allamah Yaqut also mentioned (4/225) that it is a proper noun for a village in the region of Sogd in Samarkand, and a district in Upper Egypt.
Source pages p. 235

Commentary
This passage is a proof from the Torah foretelling the coming of Prophet Muhammad. It begins by quoting the verse about Mount Faran and immediately identifies Faran as Mecca by consensus. The author then explains that the phrase 'Allah came' does not mean a physical coming but rather the coming of His law and religion. Then he draws attention to the word 'isti'lan' (manifestation) used in the Torah, which implies full clarity and prominence, indicating the many miracles of Muhammad and the universal spread of his religion until the end of time. The footnote provides scholarly support for the identification of Faran as Mecca.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

Prophecy of a Prophet Like Moses from Deuteronomy
Author cites Deuteronomy prophecy of a prophet like Moses and identifies him as Muhammad.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how a specific prophecy in Deuteronomy is interpreted to foretell the coming of Prophet Muhammad.

Core Idea
A prophecy in Deuteronomy points to Muhammad as the prophet like Moses.

The author now presents a second scriptural proof, this time from the Torah. He cites a verse from Deuteronomy where God promises to raise a prophet like Moses from among the brothers of Israel. The passage identifies these 'brothers' as the Ishmaelites and concludes that only Muhammad fits the description.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once, then the translation. Notice that the author quotes directly from the Torah and then provides his interpretation.
The citation style is typical of interfaith arguments.
2
Identify the key claim
The author claims that 'brothers of the children of Israel' refers to the Ishmaelites, and that Muhammad is the only prophet sent to them after Moses.
3
Understand the reasoning
The reasoning is based on genealogical relationship: Ishmael is the brother of Isaac, and Jacob (Israel) is Isaac's son. Therefore, the Ishmaelites are the brothers of the Israelites. No prophet from Ishmael after Moses except Muhammad.
4
Reflect on the implication
Consider how this argument serves as a proof of Muhammad's prophethood from a previous scripture, showing continuity in divine revelation.
This is a common method in Islamic polemics.

Source Text

(٢) ينظر سفر التثنية ( ١٨ / ١٥ - ٢٢ ) .
And among them: what came in the fifth book of the Torah: that Allah the Exalted said to Moses (peace be upon him): 'I will raise up for the children of Israel a prophet from among their brothers like you, and I will put my words in his mouth, and he will tell them what I command him. And the man who does not accept the words of the prophet who speaks in my name, I will take vengeance on him.' And there is no doubt that the meaning of 'brothers of the children of Israel' is the children of Ishmael, since Israel (that is, Jacob) is from the offspring of Isaac, the brother of Ishmael (peace be upon them), and no prophet was sent from the offspring of Ishmael after Moses (peace be upon them both) other than our Master, our Prophet, and our Guardian Muhammad (peace be upon him). (2) Refer to Deuteronomy (18:15-22).

This passage presents another prophecy from the Torah, this time from Deuteronomy, where God promises to raise up a prophet like Moses from among the brothers of Israel. The author identifies these brothers as the descendants of Ishmael, since Ishmael was the brother of Isaac, the father of Jacob (Israel). He argues that the only prophet from Ishmael after Moses was Muhammad, thus fulfilling the prophecy.

Prophecy of Ishmael's Descendant in Genesis
Author cites Genesis prophecy about Hagar's offspring and applies it to Muhammad.

Learning Objective

Core Idea
A prophecy in Genesis points uniquely to Muhammad.

In this passage, the author draws on the Torah (Genesis) to show that a prophecy about Hagar's offspring foretells the coming of a prophet whose influence would be universal. This argument uses scriptural evidence from prior revelations to support the prophethood of Muhammad. Let's examine the passage and its interpretation.

Prophecy of the Paraclete in the Gospel
Author cites Gospel prophecies of the Paraclete and identifies him as the spirit of truth.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the author uses Gospel prophecies about the Paraclete to support the prophethood of Muhammad.

Core Idea
The Paraclete is the spirit of truth and certainty—Prophet Muhammad.

The author continues his proof of prophethood by citing Christian scriptures. He quotes two passages from the Gospel of John where Jesus promises a Paraclete. He then identifies the Paraclete as 'the spirit of truth and certainty,' pointing to a specific interpretation that aligns with Islamic belief about Prophet Muhammad.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analysis, then read the literal translation. Let the text's rhythm come through.
Notice the two separate citations from different Gospel chapters.
2
Identify the two Gospel citations
Find the references to chapters 14 and 15. What does Jesus promise in each?
3
Understand the author's identification
The author states that the Paraclete is 'the spirit of truth and certainty.' Reflect on how this relates to Muhammad.
This interpretation is key for the author's argument.
4
Examine the textual note on nunation
The author notes that 'Paraclete' appears with nunation in some copies, which may imply it is an indefinite noun. How does this support the interpretation?
The note refers to a commentary for further detail.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ومنها : ما جاء في المصحف الرابع عشر من الإنجيل : ( أنا أطلب لكم إلى أبي حتى يمنحكم ويعطيكم بارقليط (٢) ؛ ليكون معكم إلى الأبد ) (٣) ، والبارقليط : روح الحق واليقين . وفي الخامس عشر من الإنجيل : ( فأما بارقليط روح القدس الذي يرسله أبي باسمي هو يعلمكم ويمنحكم جميع الأشياء ، وهو (٢) ) (٣) . (٢) كذا في النسخ منونا مع كونه ممنوعا من الصرف للعلمية والعجمة ، فلعله في هذا الموضع أراد التنكير ؛ أي: روحا هاديا للحق ، وانظر شرح المواهب اللدنية ( ٤٢٣/٨ ) . (٣) انظر إنجيل يوحنا ( ١٦/١٤ ـ ١٧ )
And among them: what came in the fourteenth chapter of the Gospel: 'I will ask the Father for you, so that He may grant you and give you a Paraclete (2), to be with you forever' (3). (2) Thus it is in the copies with nunation, despite being indeclinable due to being a proper noun and non-Arabic; perhaps in this context he intended indefiniteness, i.e., a spirit guiding to truth. This passage presents prophecies from the Gospel of John about the Paraclete. The author cites both John 14 and John 15, identifying the Paraclete as 'the spirit of truth and certainty.' This supports the interpretation that the Paraclete refers to Muhammad, as he is the spirit of truth and certainty.

The Torah Prophecy of Hagar's Offspring
Sanusi cites a Torah prophecy about Hagar's offspring and applies it to Muhammad.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Sanusi interprets a Torah prophecy about Hagar's offspring as a proof of Muhammad's prophethood.

Core Idea
A Torah promise of universal dominion points directly to Muhammad.

Sanusi begins by establishing a genealogical link: after Moses, no prophet came from Ishmael's line except Muhammad. He then cites a prophecy from the first book of the Torah, where God tells Abraham that Hagar's son will have a hand above all. This sets the stage for a powerful argument that only Muhammad fulfills this condition of universal authority and submission.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation once to grasp the flow from genealogy to prophecy to interpretation.
Notice how Sanusi moves from a historical claim to a scriptural citation and then to its application.
2
Understand the genealogical premise
Sanusi states that Israel (Jacob) is from Isaac, brother of Ishmael, and that after Moses, no prophet came from Ishmael's line except Muhammad. This premise narrows the candidates for the prophecy.
This genealogy is crucial for identifying the offspring referred to in the prophecy.
3
Identify the prophecy and its interpretation
The Torah says Hagar will bear a son whose hand is above all, and all hands are extended to him in humility. Sanusi argues that this can only be Muhammad, because his mission was universal and his religion prevailed over all.
The key phrase is 'hand above all' (يد فوق الجميع), interpreted as universal dominion and submission of all people.
4
Reflect on the supporting evidence
Sanusi reinforces the interpretation by noting that God made Muhammad's religion prevail over all others, and all people submitted to him in humility. This matches the prophecy's description.
5
Review key terms
Familiarize yourself with the Arabic terms for 'first book of the Torah', 'Hagar', and 'Ishmael', as they appear in the passage.
These terms help identify the scriptural source and the figures involved.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
إذ إسرائيل - وهو يعقوب - مِنْ ولدِ إسحاق أخي إسماعيل عليهم الصلاة والسلام ، ولم يُبْعَثْ مِنْ ولد إسماعيل بعد موسى عليهما الصلاة والسلام غير سيدنا ونبينا ومولانا محمَّدٍ صَلَّى اللهُ عليهِ وسلَّمَ .
ومنها : ما جاءَ في السِّفرِ الأَوَّلِ مِنَ التوراة : أَنَّهُ تعالى قالَ لإبراهيم عليه الصلاةُ والسلام : ( إِنَّ هاجَرَ تَلِدُ ، ويكونُ مِنْ ولدِها مَنْ تكون يده فوق الجميع ، ويد الجميع مبسوطة إليه بالخشوع ) ، ولا خفاءَ أنَّه لم يكنْ مِنْ ولدِ هاجرَ مَنْ يدُهُ فوق الجميع غير نبينا ومولانا محمَّدٍ صلَّى اللهُ عليهِ وسلَّمَ ؛ فإِنَّهُ بُعِثَ إِلَى أَهْلِ الأَرضِ كافة ، وأظهر الله تعالى دينه على الأديان كلّها ، وأذعن له جميع أهل الأرض ، وبسطوا إليهِ أيديهم بالذِّلَّةِ والخشوع .
ومنها : ما جاء في المصحفِ الرابعَ عشرَ مِنَ الإنجيل : ( أنا أطلب لكم إلى أبي حتى يمنحكم ويعطيكم بارقليط ؛ ليكون معكم إلى الأبد ) ، والبارقليط : روح الحق واليقين .
وفي الخامس عشرَ مِنَ الإنجيل : ( فأمَّا بارقليط روح القدس الذي يرسله أبي باسمي هو يعلمكم ويمنحكم جميع الأشياء ، وهو يُذكِّرُكم ما قلتُ لكم ) ، ثمَّ قال : ( وإني أخبرتكم بهذا قبل أن يكون ؛ حتى إذا كان ذلك تؤمنون به )
وقوله : ( أبي ) معناه : ربي وإلهي ، وقوله : ( باسمي ) يعني : بالنبوَّة مثلي ، ومعنى ( البارقليط ) : النبي كاشف الخفيَّاتِ و معنى كونِهِ رُوحَ الحقِّ واليقين والقسط الذي هو العدلُ : أنَّ هذهِ الأشياء قبل مبعث نبينا ومولانا محمَّدٍ صَلَّى اللهُ عليهِ وسلَّمَ كالميتِ لا حراك لها ولا انتعاش ، ونبينا عليه الصلاة والسلامُ إِذا بُعِثَ هو كالروح لها ، فترجعُ حينئذ قائمةً في الأرضِ ، ولا خفاءَ أَنَّهُ عليهِ الصلاة والسلام هو الذي أحيا الله تعالى به بعد عيسى عليه الصلاة والسلام الحق واليقين والعدل بعدَما خَمَدَتْ وماتَتْ ، وانتشر الباطلُ وقوي أمرُه ، وهو عليه الصلاة والسلام الذي يبقى شرعُهُ معَ الخَلْقِ إلى الأبد
وفي المصحفِ السادس عشرَ مِنَ الإنجيل : ( أقولُ لكمُ الآنَ حقاً يقيناً : إن انطلاقي عنكم خير لكم ، فإن لم أنطلق عنكم إلى أبي لم يأتِكم البارقليط ، وإن انطلقتُ أرسلتُ بهِ إليكم ، فإذا ما جاءَ هو يفيدُ أهل العالم ويدينهم ويُوبِّخُهم ويوقفُهم على الخطيئةِ والبِرِّ ) ، ثمَّ قال : ( إذا جاءَ روح الحقِّ واليقين يرشدكم ويُعلمكم ويُدبَّرُكم بجميع الخلْقِ ؛ لأنَّهُ ليسَ يَتَكَلَّمُ بدعةٌ مِنْ تلقاءِ نفسِهِ )
Since Israel—he is Jacob—is from the offspring of Isaac, the brother of Ishmael, peace be upon them, and no prophet was sent from the offspring of Ishmael after Moses, peace be upon them, except our master, our prophet, and our patron Muhammad, may God bless him and grant him peace. And among them: what came in the first book of the Torah: that He, exalted, said to Abraham, peace be upon him: 'Indeed, Hagar will give birth, and from her offspring will be one whose hand is above all, and the hand of all is extended to him in humility.' There is no doubt that there was none from the offspring of Hagar whose hand was above all except our prophet and patron Muhammad, may God bless him and grant him peace; for he was sent to all the people of the earth, and God, exalted, made His religion prevail over all religions, and all the people of the earth submitted to him, and they extended their hands to him in lowliness and humility. And among them: what came in the fourteenth chapter of the Gospel: 'I ask the Father for you until He grants you and gives you a Paraclete, to be with you forever.' And the Paraclete is the spirit of truth and certainty. And in the fifteenth chapter of the Gospel: 'But the Paraclete, the Holy Spirit, whom the Father will send in my name, He will teach you and grant you all things, and He will remind you of what I said to you.' Then he said: 'And I have told you this before it happens, so that when it happens you may believe in it.' And His saying: 'My Father' means: my Lord and my God, and His saying: 'in my name' means: in prophethood like me. And the meaning of 'the Paraclete' is: the prophet who reveals hidden things. And the meaning of his being the spirit of truth and certainty and justice, which is equity: that these things before the mission of our prophet and patron Muhammad, may God bless him and grant him peace, were like dead, having no movement or revival, and our prophet, peace be upon him, when he was sent, is like the spirit for them, so they then return standing in the earth. There is no doubt that he, peace be upon him, is the one through whom God, exalted, after Jesus, peace be upon him, revived truth, certainty, and justice after they had faded and died, and falsehood spread and its matter became strong, and he, peace be upon him, is the one whose law will remain with creation forever. And in the sixteenth chapter of the Gospel: 'I tell you now truly and certainly: my departure from you is better for you, for if I do not depart from you to the Father, the Paraclete will not come to you; but if I depart, I will send him to you. And when he comes, he will benefit the people of the world, and judge them, and rebuke them, and make them stand upon sin and righteousness.' Then he said: 'When the spirit of truth and certainty comes, he will guide you and teach you and manage you with all creation; for he does not speak a novelty from his own accord.'
Source pages p. 236

Commentary
This passage is a cumulative proof of Muhammad's prophethood from biblical scriptures. It begins with a historical claim: Israel (Jacob) is from Isaac, brother of Ishmael, and after Moses, no prophet came from Ishmael's line except Muhammad. This sets the stage for the prophecies. The first proof is from the Torah's first book (Genesis): God tells Abraham that Hagar will bear a son whose hand will be above all, and all hands will be extended to him in humility. The author argues this can only refer to Muhammad, as his mission was universal, his religion prevailed, and all submitted to him. The second proof is from the Gospel, citing multiple chapters about the Paraclete (بارقليط). He explains that 'Paraclete' means 'spirit of truth and certainty', and interprets it as a prophet who reveals hidden things. He then interprets the phrases 'My Father' and 'in my name' to mean 'my Lord and God' and 'in prophethood like me', respectively. The meaning of the Paraclete being the spirit of truth and justice is that before Muhammad's mission, these virtues were dead, and he revived them. Finally, a third Gospel citation emphasizes that the Paraclete will guide and judge, and will not speak from himself. The author concludes that these prophecies all point unmistakably to Muhammad.

Key Terms
السفر الأول من التوراة
هاجر
إسماعيل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think the Torah prophecy about Hagar's son refers to Ishmael himself or another prophet like Moses.
Correction: Sanusi shows that the prophecy's description of universal dominion and submission can only apply to Muhammad, as Ishmael was not a prophet sent to all people, and no other prophet from Ishmael's line after Moses had universal sway.

Checkpoint
According to Sanusi, what does the prophecy 'his hand above all' in the Torah refer to?
A) The physical strength of Ishmael
B) The universal dominion and submission to Muhammad
C) The military conquests of Moses
D) The spiritual authority of Jesus

Key Takeaway
Sanusi uses a Torah prophecy about Hagar's offspring to argue that Muhammad is the promised prophet with universal authority.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The Torah prophecy says all hands will be extended to Muhammad in humility. In which area of your life are you still withholding that humility and submission to him? Name one specific change you will make this week to extend your hand in full submission.
?
Sanusi argues that no other descendant of Hagar fits this prophecy except Muhammad. Do you truly believe that Muhammad's authority is above all—above your desires, your plans, and your relationships? If so, what is one decision you are currently facing that you need to submit to his guidance rather than your own preference?
?
This passage uses a scripture from another tradition to affirm Muhammad's prophethood. Have you ever dismissed evidence for Islam because it came from an unexpected source? What would it mean for you to accept truth wherever it appears, even if it challenges your comfort zone?

The Paraclete Prophecies in the Gospel
Sanusi interprets Gospel prophecies about the Paraclete as referring to Muhammad.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Sanusi interprets Gospel prophecies about the Paraclete as referring to Muhammad.

Core Idea
The Paraclete is a human prophet who revives truth and justice, identified as Muhammad.

Sanusi continues his biblical proofs by citing several passages from the Gospel about the Paraclete (بارقليط). He explains that the term means 'prophet who reveals hidden things' and interprets the phrases 'My Father' and 'in my name' to refer to God and prophethood respectively. Finally, he connects the Paraclete's role in reviving truth, certainty, and justice to Muhammad's mission.

Guided Path
1
Read the Gospel citations
Read the Arabic and translation of the passages from the 14th, 15th, and 16th chapters of the Gospel. Notice the repeated mention of 'Paraclete' and its attributes.
The citations are from the Gospel (likely John 14:16, 15:26, 16:7-8).
2
Understand the interpretation of 'Paraclete'
Sanusi defines 'Paraclete' (بارقليط) as 'the spirit of truth and certainty' and then explains it as 'the prophet who reveals hidden things'. This is a key identification.
Sanusi rejects the common Christian interpretation of the Paraclete as the Holy Spirit and instead sees it as a human prophet.
3
Examine the explanation of 'Father' and 'in my name'
Sanusi explains that 'My Father' means 'my Lord and God' and 'in my name' means 'in prophethood like me'. This connects the Paraclete to Muhammad, who came after Jesus.
This interpretation emphasizes the prophetic lineage and the continuation of prophethood.
4
Reflect on the revival of truth and justice
Sanusi argues that before Muhammad, truth, certainty, and justice were dead, and he revived them. The Paraclete is described as the spirit that brings these virtues back to life.
Metaphor of revival: the Paraclete is like a spirit that revives what was dead.
5
Summarize the cumulative proof
Combine the three Gospel citations and Sanusi's interpretations to see how they collectively point to Muhammad as the promised Paraclete.
The proof is cumulative; each citation adds detail that matches Muhammad's attributes.

وقوله : ( أبي ) معناه : ربي وإلهي ، وقوله : ( باسمي ) يعني : بالنبوَّة مثلي ، ومعنى ( البارقليط ) : النبي كاشف الخفيَّاتِ و معنى كونِهِ رُوحَ الحقِّ واليقين والقسط الذي هو العدلُ : أنَّ هذهِ الأشياء قبل مبعث نبينا ومولانا محمَّدٍ صَلَّى اللهُ عليهِ وسلَّمَ كالميتِ لا حراك لها ولا انتعاش ، ونبينا عليه الصلاة والسلامُ إِذا بُعِثَ هو كالروح لها ، فترجعُ حينئذ قائمةً في الأرضِ ، ولا خفاءَ أَنَّهُ عليهِ الصلاة والسلام هو الذي أحيا الله تعالى به بعد عيسى عليه الصلاة والسلام الحق واليقين والعدل بعدَما خَمَدَتْ وماتَتْ ، وانتشر الباطلُ وقوي أمرُه ، وهو عليه الصلاة والسلام الذي يبقى شرعُهُ معَ الخَلْقِ إلى الأبد

Since Israel—he is Jacob—is from the offspring of Isaac, the brother of Ishmael, peace be upon them, and no prophet was sent from the offspring of Ishmael after Moses, peace be upon them, except our master, our prophet, and our patron Muhammad, may God bless him and grant him peace. And among them: what came in the first book of the Torah: that He, exalted, said to Abraham, peace be upon him: 'Indeed, Hagar will give birth, and from her offspring will be one whose hand is above all, and the hand of all is extended to him in humility.' There is no doubt that there was none from the offspring of Hagar whose hand was above all except our prophet and patron Muhammad, may God bless him and grant him peace; for he was sent to all the people of the earth, and God, exalted, made His religion prevail over all religions, and all the people of the earth submitted to him, and they extended their hands to him in lowliness and humility. And among them: what came in the fourteenth chapter of the Gospel: 'I ask the Father for you until He grants you and gives you a Paraclete, to be with you forever.' And the Paraclete is the spirit of truth and certainty. And in the fifteenth chapter of the Gospel: 'But the Paraclete, the Holy Spirit, whom the Father will send in my name, He will teach you and grant you all things, and He will remind you of what I said to you.' Then he said: 'And I have told you this before it happens, so that when it happens you may believe in it.' And His saying: 'My Father' means: my Lord and my God, and His saying: 'in my name' means: in prophethood like me. And the meaning of 'the Paraclete' is: the prophet who reveals hidden things. And the meaning of his being the spirit of truth and certainty and justice, which is equity: that these things before the mission of our prophet and patron Muhammad, may God bless him and grant him peace, were like dead, having no movement or revival, and our prophet, peace be upon him, when he was sent, is like the spirit for them, so they then return standing in the earth. There is no doubt that he, peace be upon him, is the one through whom God, exalted, after Jesus, peace be upon him, revived truth, certainty, and justice after they had faded and died, and falsehood spread and its matter became strong, and he, peace be upon him, is the one whose law will remain with creation forever. And in the sixteenth chapter of the Gospel: 'I tell you now truly and certainly: my departure from you is better for you, for if I do not depart from you to the Father, the Paraclete will not come to you; but if I depart, I will send him to you. And when he comes, he will benefit the people of the world, and judge them, and rebuke them, and make them stand upon sin and righteousness.' Then he said: 'When the spirit of truth and certainty comes, he will guide you and teach you and manage you with all creation; for he does not speak a novelty from his own accord.'
Source pages p. 236

Commentary
This passage is a cumulative proof of Muhammad's prophethood from biblical scriptures. It begins with a historical claim: Israel (Jacob) is from Isaac, brother of Ishmael, and after Moses, no prophet came from Ishmael's line except Muhammad. This sets the stage for the prophecies. The first proof is from the Torah's first book (Genesis): God tells Abraham that Hagar will bear a son whose hand will be above all, and all hands will be extended to him in humility. The author argues this can only refer to Muhammad, as his mission was universal, his religion prevailed, and all submitted to him. The second proof is from the Gospel, citing multiple chapters about the Paraclete (بارقليط). He explains that 'Paraclete' means 'spirit of truth and certainty', and interprets it as a prophet who reveals hidden things. He then interprets the phrases 'My Father' and 'in my name' to mean 'my Lord and God' and 'in prophethood like me', respectively. The meaning of the Paraclete being the spirit of truth and justice is that before Muhammad's mission, these virtues were dead, and he revived them. Finally, a third Gospel citation emphasizes that the Paraclete will guide and judge, and will not speak from himself. The author concludes that these prophecies all point unmistakably to Muhammad.

Key Terms
بارقليط
روح الحق
المصحف الرابع عشر من الإنجيل
النبي كاشف الخفيَّات

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The Paraclete is commonly understood by Christians as the Holy Spirit.
Correction: Sanusi argues that the Paraclete is a human prophet, specifically Muhammad, as he is the one who revealed hidden things and revived truth and justice.

Checkpoint
According to Sanusi, what does the term 'Paraclete' (بارقليط) mean in the Gospel prophecies?
A) The Holy Spirit
B) The prophet who reveals hidden things
C) A divine attribute
D) The Messiah

Key Takeaway
Sanusi interprets the Gospel's Paraclete prophecies as unmistakable signs pointing to Muhammad as a prophet who revives truth and justice.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?
Jesus said the Paraclete will remind you of what I said. How does this challenge you to reconsider the continuity of God's message across prophets?
?
The author states that before Muhammad, truth and certainty were like dead. In what ways does this passage call you to value the revival brought by Muhammad more deeply in your daily practice?

The Paraclete as Prophet: Al-Barqalīt and Revival of Truth
Sanusi defines al-Barqalīt and argues Muhammad revived truth.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Sanusi's interpretation of the term 'al-Barqalīt' as a prophet who reveals hidden things and his claim that Prophet Muhammad revived truth, certainty, and justice after they had become dead.

Core Idea
Al-Barqalīt is not a spirit but a prophet who revives dead truth.

Sanusi begins by unpacking a Gospel statement about the Paraclete. He defines the key terms and then makes a bold claim: before Muhammad, truth and justice were lifeless. Muhammad brought them back to life like a spirit reviving a dead body. This lesson explores that argument.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage on the Paraclete
Read the Arabic passage where Sanusi defines 'al-Barqalīt' and explains its connection to truth, certainty, and justice. Notice how he uses the metaphor of revival.
The phrase 'as a spirit for them' (كالروح لها) is central.
2
Understand the definition of al-Barqalīt
Sanusi says al-Barqalīt means 'the prophet who reveals hidden things'. Reflect on why this fits Prophet Muhammad.
This is a direct refutation of the Christian interpretation as Holy Spirit.
3
Follow the revival argument
Identify the claim that truth, certainty, and justice were dead before Muhammad, and that he revived them. Note the contrast between life and falsehood.
Sanusi emphasizes that Muhammad's law remains forever.

And his sending our Prophet, may God bless him and grant him peace: it is possible that its meaning is that he causes that by his desire to God Most High, and it is possible that it is because he, peace and blessings be upon him, knew that the mission of our Master and our Prophet Muhammad, may God bless him and grant him peace, would only be after his ascension and his disappearance from people, and that his ascension is among the signs of his mission, may God bless him and grant him peace. So he attributed the sending to himself in this sense metaphorically, and God Most High knows best.

Commentary
This passage presents Sanusi's interpretation of Gospel prophecies about the Paraclete (al-Barqalīt) as referring to Prophet Muhammad. It begins by citing a verse from the Gospel where Jesus says he will send the Paraclete, then explains that 'My Father' means God, 'in my name' means in prophethood. The key term 'al-Barqalīt' is defined as the prophet who reveals hidden things. Sanusi then explains that truth, certainty, and justice before Muhammad were dead, and Muhammad revived them. He cites John 16:7-14, where Jesus speaks of sending the Paraclete who will guide and rebuke. Sanusi interprets this as referring to Muhammad. Finally, he discusses the meaning of Jesus' 'departure' to his Father, offering two possibilities: either Jesus asked God to send Muhammad, or Jesus knew that his ascension would be a sign of Muhammad's coming, so he metaphorically attributed the sending to himself.

Jesus' Departure and Sending of Muhammad
Sanusi interprets John 16 and explains Jesus' departure and sending of Muhammad.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Sanusi's interpretation of Jesus' statement about departing and sending the Paraclete, including two possibilities for how Jesus 'sent' Muhammad.

Core Idea
Jesus' departure heralds Muhammad's coming, not a spirit.

This lesson continues Sanusi's interpretation of the Gospel. He cites John 16, where Jesus says he must depart for the Paraclete to come. Sanusi explains Jesus' departure as going to God, and offers two interpretations of how Jesus sent Muhammad: either by asking God directly, or metaphorically because his ascension was a sign of Muhammad's mission.

Guided Path
1
Read the Gospel citation in the passage
Read the Arabic text of John 16:7-14 as quoted by Sanusi. Notice the emphasis on departure and the coming of the Paraclete.
The key phrase is 'if I do not depart, the Paraclete will not come.'
2
Understand the meaning of 'departure'
Sanusi explains that Jesus' departure to his Father means going to the place of his elevation and honor, and turning to God.
This is not a physical death but ascension.
3
Examine the two interpretations of sending
Sanusi gives two possibilities: either Jesus asked God to send Muhammad, or Jesus' ascension was a sign, so he attributed the sending to himself metaphorically.
Both interpretations assert that the Paraclete is Muhammad.

بالنبوَّة مثلي ، ومعنى ( البارقليط ) : النبي كاشف الخفيَّاتِ و معنى كونِهِ رُوحَ الحقِّ واليقين والقسط الذي هو العدلُ : أنَّ هذهِ الأشياء قبل مبعث نبينا ومولانا محمَّدٍ صَلَّى اللهُ عليهِ وسلَّمَ كالميتِ لا حراك لها ولا انتعاش ، ونبينا عليه الصلاة والسلامُ إِذا بُعِثَ هو كالروح لها ، فترجعُ حينئذ قائمةً في الأرضِ ، ولا خفاءَ أَنَّهُ عليهِ الصلاة والسلام هو الذي أحيا الله تعالى به بعد عيسى عليه الصلاة والسلام الحق واليقين والعدل بعدَما خَمَدَتْ وماتَتْ ، وانتشر الباطلُ وقوي أمرُه ، وهو عليه الصلاة والسلام الذي يبقى شرعُهُ معَ الخَلْقِ إلى الأبد
وفي المصحفِ السادس عشرَ مِنَ الإنجيل : ( أقولُ لكمُ الآنَ حقاً يقيناً : إن انطلاقي عنكم خير لكم ، فإن لم أنطلق عنكم إلى أبي لم يأتِكم البارقليط ، وإن انطلقتُ أرسلتُ بهِ إليكم ، فإذا ما جاءَ هو يفيدُ أهل العالم ويدينهم ويُوبِّخُهم ويوقفُهم على الخطيئةِ والبِرِّ ) ، ثمَّ قال : ( إذا جاءَ روح الحقِّ واليقين يرشدكم ويُعلمكم ويُدبَّرُكم بجميع الخلْقِ ؛ لأنَّهُ ليسَ يَتَكَلَّمُ بدعةٌ مِنْ تلقاءِ نفسِهِ ((٢)

ومعنى انطلاقِ عيسى عليهِ السلامُ إلى أبيه ؛ أي : ربِّهِ عزَّ وجلَّ : انطلاقه إلى محلّ رفعتِهِ وكرامتِهِ ، والاستراحةُ مِنَ الناسِ ، والتوجه بكُليَّةِ القلبِ إلى المولى تبارك وتعالى ، وكونه يرسل نبينا صلَّى اللهُ عليهِ وسلَّمَ : يحتمل أن يكون معناه : أَنَّهُ يَتسبب في ذلكَ برغبتِهِ إلى الله تعالى ، ويحتملُ أنْ يكونَ : لمَّا عَلِمَ عليه الصلاةُ والسلامُ أَنَّ بعْثَ سيّدِنا ومولانا محمَّدٍ صلى الله عليهِ وسلَّمَ إِنَّما يكون بعد رفعه وتغييبِهِ مِنَ الناسِ ، وأنَّ رفعَهُ مِنْ أماراتِ بعثِهِ صلَّى اللهُ عليهِ وسلَّمَ. . فأسند إرساله إلى نفسه بهذا المعنى على سبيل المجاز ، والله تعالى
أعلم.
"He will remind you of what I said to you", then he said: "And I have told you this before it happens, so that when it happens you may believe." (1) And his saying: "My Father" means my Lord and my God, and his saying: "in my name" means in prophethood like me, and the meaning of 'al-Barqalīt' is the prophet who reveals hidden things. And the meaning of his being the Spirit of truth, certainty, and justice which is equity: that these things before the mission of our Prophet and Master Muhammad, may God bless him and grant him peace, were like a dead thing, without movement or revival, and our Prophet, peace and blessings be upon him, when he was sent, he is like the spirit for them, so they return then standing on earth. And it is no secret that he, peace and blessings be upon him, is the one through whom God Most High revived after Jesus, peace and blessings be upon him, truth, certainty, and justice after they had faded and died, and falsehood spread and its matter became strong, and he, peace and blessings be upon him, is the one whose law remains with creation forever. And in the sixteenth chapter of the Gospel: "I say to you now truly, certainly: My departure from you is better for you, for if I do not depart from you to My Father, the Paraclete will not come to you; but if I depart, I will send him to you. And when he comes, he will benefit the people of the world and judge them and rebuke them and make them stand upon sin and righteousness." Then he said: "When the Spirit of truth and certainty comes, he will guide you and teach you and manage you with all creation; because he does not speak innovation from himself." (2) (1) See the Gospel of John, 14:26-29. (2) See the Gospel of John, 16:7-14. And the meaning of the departure of Jesus, peace be upon him, to his Father, i.e., his Lord, Mighty and Majestic: his departure to the place of his elevation and honor, and rest from people, and turning with the whole heart to the Master, Blessed and Most High. And his sending our Prophet, may God bless him and grant him peace: it is possible that its meaning is that he causes that by his desire to God Most High, and it is possible that it is because he, peace and blessings be upon him, knew that the mission of our Master and our Prophet Muhammad, may God bless him and grant him peace, would only be after his ascension and his disappearance from people, and that his ascension is among the signs of his mission, may God bless him and grant him peace. So he attributed the sending to himself in this sense metaphorically, and God Most High knows best.
Source pages p. 237

Commentary
This passage presents Sanusi's interpretation of Gospel prophecies about the Paraclete (al-Barqalīt) as referring to Prophet Muhammad. It begins by citing a verse from the Gospel where Jesus says he will send the Paraclete, then explains that 'My Father' means God, 'in my name' means in prophethood. The key term 'al-Barqalīt' is defined as the prophet who reveals hidden things. Sanusi then explains that truth, certainty, and justice before Muhammad were dead, and Muhammad revived them. He cites John 16:7-14, where Jesus speaks of sending the Paraclete who will guide and rebuke. Sanusi interprets this as referring to Muhammad. Finally, he discusses the meaning of Jesus' 'departure' to his Father, offering two possibilities: either Jesus asked God to send Muhammad, or Jesus knew that his ascension would be a sign of Muhammad's coming, so he metaphorically attributed the sending to himself.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think the Paraclete comes only after Jesus' physical death and is a spirit.
Correction: Sanusi interprets the Paraclete as Muhammad, and Jesus' departure as ascension, not death.

Checkpoint
According to Sanusi, what is one possible meaning of Jesus 'sending' the Paraclete?
A) Jesus physically sent him after his death
B) Jesus interceded with God to send him
C) Jesus himself became the Paraclete
D) The Paraclete is the Holy Spirit

Key Takeaway
Sanusi interprets Jesus' departure as his ascension and the sending of the Paraclete as the coming of Prophet Muhammad, either by Jesus' request or metaphorically.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You claim to believe that Muhammad is the fulfillment of Jesus' prophecy. Does your daily life demonstrate that you have received a guide who teaches and rebukes you, or do you live as if you are still waiting for that guide?
?
If you truly believe that Jesus' departure was for your benefit so that Muhammad could come, then how do you treat the message of Muhammad? Do you accept his teachings with the same urgency as the early disciples?
?
Sanusi offers two interpretations of Jesus' statement 'I will send him.' One is that Jesus interceded with God, the other that he spoke metaphorically. Which one makes you feel greater gratitude to Jesus? And how does that gratitude change the way you relate to both prophets?
